  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6e992da5-8e15-4583-9d8e-8bed079473f9'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1309128986" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14744268362372016487"><a href='http://heradas.com/2018/06/burning-chrome-by-william-gibson-cyberpunk-as-an-alternate-now/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Posted at Heradas</a><br/><br/>William Gibson blew the Science Fiction world wide open in the mid eighties with his cyberpunk novels, particularly the Hugo, Nebula, and Philip K. Dick award winning <a href='https://amzn.to/2JE8wDz' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neuromancer</a>. Ridley Scott gave us the visual aesthetic with <a href='https://amzn.to/2Js71Fi' rel='nofollow noopener'>Blade Runner</a>, but Gibson firmly established Cyberpunk as a literary movement. As a genre it would go on to live a fairly short life, plateauing in the late eighties, followed by a handful of peak post-cyberpunk moments in the nineties (<a href='https://amzn.to/2JnFp8v' rel='nofollow noopener'>Snow Crash</a>, <a href='https://amzn.to/2JE8V95' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ghost in</a></span>
  <span id="freeText14744268362372016487" style="display:none"><a href="http://heradas.com/2018/06/burning-chrome-by-william-gibson-cyberpunk-as-an-alternate-now/" rel="nofollow noopener">Posted at Heradas</a><br><br>William Gibson blew the Science Fiction world wide open in the mid eighties with his cyberpunk novels, particularly the Hugo, Nebula, and Philip K. Dick award winning <a href="https://amzn.to/2JE8wDz" rel="nofollow noopener">Neuromancer</a>. Ridley Scott gave us the visual aesthetic with <a href="https://amzn.to/2Js71Fi" rel="nofollow noopener">Blade Runner</a>, but Gibson firmly established Cyberpunk as a literary movement. As a genre it would go on to live a fairly short life, plateauing in the late eighties, followed by a handful of peak post-cyberpunk moments in the nineties (<a href="https://amzn.to/2JnFp8v" rel="nofollow noopener">Snow Crash</a>, <a href="https://amzn.to/2JE8V95" rel="nofollow noopener">Ghost in the Shell</a>) culminating in <a href="https://amzn.to/2JktEzq" rel="nofollow noopener">The Matrix</a> and then almost immediately fading into relative obscurity.<br><br>Burning Chrome collects Gibson’s short fiction, mostly published in OMNI magazine in the early eighties. Unlike a lot of short fiction collections, this one isn’t a totally mixed bag, most of the stories ranging from “good” to “great”. Three of them, (Johnny Mnemonic, New Rose Hotel, and Burning Chrome) are set in the same universe as Gibson’s Sprawl series (<a href="https://amzn.to/2JE8wDz" rel="nofollow noopener">Neuromancer</a>, <a href="https://amzn.to/2JpZGtV" rel="nofollow noopener">Count Zero</a>, <a href="https://amzn.to/2sQDhLf" rel="nofollow noopener">Mona Lisa Overdrive</a>), with some of their characters and events mentioned or referenced in the main novels.<br><br><a href="https://amzn.to/2JqlKAI" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528474975i/25689152.jpg" alt="William Gibson" width="250" height="286" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>As a rambling aside, I was born in 1984, making me much too young to have experienced cyberpunk when it was new and revolutionary. When I read cyberpunk now, nearing the third decade of the twenty-first century, it’s hard to think of it as anything other than a form of retrofuturism. The technological tropes of the genre were one possible direction for things to head. In reality, they didn’t. Instead of the cumbersome and clunky virtual reality user interfaces, and “jacking in”, we went with the more boring, economical methods: a little white search box on a screen, and fingers on keys or key analogs. Just as space opera was written back before anybody knew the realities of space travel, cyberpunk was written before anyone knew the realities of the internet. All we knew was that it was going to change everything, no one knew how exactly.<br><br>There are of course still giant multinational evil corporations, another trope of the genre, but instead of inspiring monolithic dread, they have friendly, marketable faces. We all know that <a href="http://www.businessinsider.com/amazon-warehouse-workers-have-to-pee-into-bottles-2018-4" rel="nofollow noopener">Amazon is terrible to their employees</a>, and drives small businesses into the ground, but it’s just so.. convenient. We all know that our devices are made by slaves, but those new <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=J2m7EUwnqGg" rel="nofollow noopener">animated emoji</a> are just so cute. In the twenty-first century we adore the giant evil corporation. We buy their devices to put in our homes and listen to our conversations, so it’ll be easier to order things with our voices. Convenience kills us, slowly but assuredly.<br><br><b>“It was hot, the night we burned Chrome.”</b><br><br>This current drowning-in-technology age has brought with it a sort of resurgence in, not specifically cyberpunk, but a more modern interpretation of its core theme: alienation among ubiquities connection. Being alone together. Television shows like <a href="https://amzn.to/2xUgN1x" rel="nofollow noopener">Black Mirror</a> embody this better than anything else currently around. There has also been a cyberpunk resurgence in pop culture, specifically music, visible primarily in the retrowave / synthwave / “outrun” genre of electronic music. Retrowave is a <a href="https://amzn.to/2JkTK5n" rel="nofollow noopener">Baudrillardian</a> simulacra of eighties electronic music usually created by those too young to have first-hand experience of eighties culture. It’s created with postmodern sensibilities, as an idea of what eighties culture could’ve been, not what it actually was. A sort of copy of a copy of some collective idea that never existed, except in the imaginings of its purveyors. Highly influenced by multimedia created in the eighties, specifically science fiction and horror films and books, it’s dark and menacing, and accompanies with it a mental image of dystopic science fictional landscapes. It’s the aural version of cyberpunk.<br><br>I say all of this because I think right now, with all of these elements converging, is the perfect time to read cyberpunk literature. Our temporal distance from its inception gives us the perspective to appreciate it as retrofuturism, and the relational closeness of the technological and emotional aspects of our lives to its themes, creates this nebulous landscape that makes it highly relatable to our modern moment. Our technology makes us chrome and neon and dark inside, but externally we’re living it up on social media while we collectively experience a sort of soul-death alone behind our screens, our modern day mirrorshades.<br><br>It’s a weird ass world, and these stories give us a glimpse into a stranger one that could’ve been, had just a few things played out differently. Cyberpunk is an alternate now.<br><br>High points: The Hinterlands, New Rose Hotel, and Burning Chrome<br><br>Low Points: Johnny Mnemonic, Red Star, Winter Orbit<br><br><strong>Johnny Mnemonic: 2/5</strong><br>I find Gibson’s writing very difficult to digest here. The structure of the writing is unlike anything else I’ve read, and I think it’s safe to say that no one quite saw the world the was that he did. Weird concepts, weird execution. Set in the Sprawl universe. This was adapted into the not-so-great Keanu Reeves film in the mid nineties.<br><br><a href="https://amzn.to/2sScWwe" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528474975i/25689153.jpg" alt="Johnny Mnemonic" width="206" height="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><strong>The Gernsback Continuum: 4/5</strong><br>A man gets stuck in an idea of a future 1980s that never was. Built out of the retrofuturism of 1930s design. Great little paranoid Philip K. Dickian story.<br><br><strong>Fragments of a Hologram Rose: 3/5</strong><br>A melancholy take on lost love and being out of place in the world. The ASP tech is a cool idea, and it seems to be the analogue of reading someone's journal, in a sense.<br><br><strong>The Belonging Kind: 3/5</strong><br>Nice analog for social anxiety, it was fun but nothing special.<br><br><strong>The Hinterlands: 5/5</strong><br>This is a new personal favorite. It’s written in exquisitely beautiful prose, and has such a unique story, like nothing I've ever read. I actually read it twice in a row, because I was initially confused by the first few sections. It all became much clearer with the accompanying context of the rest of the story. A great little tale about loss, alien contact, and psychology. I loved it.<br><br><strong>Red Star, Winter Orbit: 1/5</strong><br>Very difficult to follow, kind of boring.<br><br><strong>New Rose Hotel: 5/5</strong><br>A great corporate espionage noir. For me, this is the definitive high-tech low-life story. It was adapted into the eponymous Christopher Walken/Willem Dafoe film by Abel Ferrara in 1998. Set in the Sprawl universe.<br><br><a href="https://amzn.to/2sGATrk" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528474975i/25689154.jpg" alt="New Rose Hotel" width="216" height="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><strong>The Winter Market: 4/5</strong><br>Entertainment in an age where we've moved beyond the audiovisual form, and into something much stranger. I enjoyed the existentialism.<br><br><strong>Dogfight: 3/5</strong><br>A drifter on the way to Florida picks up a competitive VR game, and befriends a college student.<br><br><strong>Burning Chrome: 4/5</strong><br>The most ‘Cyberpunk’ story in the collection. Set in the Sprawl universe.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14744268362372016487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer3300533320606829187">If you're into stuff like this, <a href='http://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1992/09/future-technologies-burning-chrome-by.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>you can read the full review.</a><br/><br/><br/>Future Technologies: "Burning Chrome" by William Gibson<br/><br/><br/><br/>Hippies have known about these dangerous technologies for a long time, and the state cracks down hard on them, and not entirely without good reason either. The world cannot run (for long anyway) on raves and drugs and loud music, any fool can see that. There is also a false economy in these supposedly 'efficient' economies, because if you run a sustainable event and people attend y</span>
  <span id="freeText3300533320606829187" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="http://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1992/09/future-technologies-burning-chrome-by.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br />Future Technologies: "Burning Chrome" by William Gibson<br /><br /><br /><br />Hippies have known about these dangerous technologies for a long time, and the state cracks down hard on them, and not entirely without good reason either. The world cannot run (for long anyway) on raves and drugs and loud music, any fool can see that. There is also a false economy in these supposedly 'efficient' economies, because if you run a sustainable event and people attend your event in a car, you can wave goodbye to any benefits you might have yielded from the technology itself. The ubiquity of the car, and all the other rampant wasteful consumerism which often surrounds an event, cripples your efforts right from the off. This thinking is very much in a fledgling stage and has a long way to develop before it could be said to be anything more than purely experimental, but experiment we must.</span>

<div id="review_2543663101" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2543663101?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2543663101" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10485055767865537943">I jacked into the Toronto construct matrix and downloaded Gibson’s Burning Chrome onto my deck, plugged the simstim recorder on and zoned out.<br/><br/>Gibson’s Sprawl collection from eighty-six was as lethal as black ice, but my neural implants would keep me dosing through all ten shorts. A handful of amitriptyline and a slug of Sobieski would also keep me running. Gibson is at his best in the short medium and I wanted to catch it all.<br/><br/>Some stories shine on BAMA and others are stand-alones, but all good. </span>
  <span id="freeText10485055767865537943" style="display:none">I jacked into the Toronto construct matrix and downloaded Gibson’s Burning Chrome onto my deck, plugged the simstim recorder on and zoned out.<br><br>Gibson’s Sprawl collection from eighty-six was as lethal as black ice, but my neural implants would keep me dosing through all ten shorts. A handful of amitriptyline and a slug of Sobieski would also keep me running. Gibson is at his best in the short medium and I wanted to catch it all.<br><br>Some stories shine on BAMA and others are stand-alones, but all good. Last summer when I took the glider into Kiev to torch the Maas Biolabs, I thought about Molly and "Johnny Mnemonic", Gibson’s anthem blaring like Lado-Acheson. Stories like "Dogfight" and the "Belonging Kind" show that William can collaborate. "Red Star, Winter Orbit" is a tasty alternate history where the rooskies won the space race.<br><br>"The Gernsback Continuum" reminds me of Gibson’s golden age roots and also a Bradburyesque salute.<br><br>Two sticks of dex gets me to Freeside and a meeting with the Yakuza rep. A Tessier-Ashpool goon smashes the deck under his jack boot but I was already done, Gibson’s smooth prose gliding through the pages like chrome.<br><br>Now it’s off to Chiba City where I think I can find Molly.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1540263162i/26486384._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="225" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/911544967?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer911544967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18044600788415652886"><i>“if poets are the unacknowledged legislators of the world, science-fiction writers are its court jesters.”</i> <br/>― Bruce Sterling introducing William Gibson's, Burning Chrome<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397712165i/9316522.jpg' width='340' height='264' alt='russian matrix' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>A set of 10 short stories: early Gibson cyberpunk and sic-fi that anticipate both his SPRAWL and BLUE ANT series. All the Gibson tropes are there just waiting to bud and bloom. Gibson's cyberpunk, dark and messy near-future; his obsession with technology, music, clothing; his uncanny ability to describe and name the bleeding edge </span>
  <span id="freeText18044600788415652886" style="display:none"><i>“if poets are the unacknowledged legislators of the world, science-fiction writers are its court jesters.”</i> <br>― Bruce Sterling introducing William Gibson's, Burning Chrome<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397712165i/9316522.jpg" width="340" height="264" alt="russian matrix" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A set of 10 short stories: early Gibson cyberpunk and sic-fi that anticipate both his SPRAWL and BLUE ANT series. All the Gibson tropes are there just waiting to bud and bloom. Gibson's cyberpunk, dark and messy near-future; his obsession with technology, music, clothing; his uncanny ability to describe and name the bleeding edge where culture and technology blend; his noirish tribalism; his satire; his slick style; his curvy asians. The book is an uneven group of stories that approximate a pimply and adolescent Gibson sitting confidently on a couch ready to hack your future and steal your dated sci-fi pulp.</span>

<div id="review_18869298" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18869298" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18869298?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780441089345" href="/book/show/920338.Burning_Chrome">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=hyperreality">hyperreality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=speculative">speculative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=space-noir">space-noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=cyberpunk">cyberpunk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18869298" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9618894374728098079">Is it okay, do you think, to say I find <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/306.William_Golding' title='William Golding' rel='nofollow noopener'>William Gibson</a>'s cycle of short stories, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22323.Burning_Chrome' title='Burning Chrome by William Gibson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Burning Chrome</a>, to be a work of profound beauty? Probably not, but I'm going to say it anyway: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22323.Burning_Chrome' title='Burning Chrome by William Gibson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Burning Chrome</a> is beautiful.<br/><br/>But how can it be? How can something like the Sprawl, Gibson's pollution choked mega-city, and our shared technological-future-nightmare be beautiful? My description suggests it can't, yet I find much beauty in Gibson's future. <br/><br/>There's something magnificent about monomolecular wires and Razorgir</span>
  <span id="freeText9618894374728098079" style="display:none">Is it okay, do you think, to say I find <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/306.William_Golding" title="William Golding" rel="nofollow noopener">William Gibson</a>'s cycle of short stories, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22323.Burning_Chrome" title="Burning Chrome by William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">Burning Chrome</a>, to be a work of profound beauty? Probably not, but I'm going to say it anyway: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22323.Burning_Chrome" title="Burning Chrome by William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">Burning Chrome</a> is beautiful.<br /><br />But how can it be? How can something like the Sprawl, Gibson's pollution choked mega-city, and our shared technological-future-nightmare be beautiful? My description suggests it can't, yet I find much beauty in Gibson's future. <br /><br />There's something magnificent about monomolecular wires and Razorgirl fingernails, something profound about the rejection of a sterile utopia for a filthy sprawl, something thrilling about dreamy future-noir, something tragic about the thirst to belong for even the most peripheral people, something eerily familiar in the desire to offer the ultimate sacrifice, something nostalgic about the Soviet era trappings that are long gone, something terrifying in the prescient vision of corporate power, something hopeful in the concept of future immortality, something touching in its melancholy, and something comfortable about improvements that can't hide a classic love story of the "if-you-love-her-let-her-go" kind.<br /><br />Well...I'm a guy who loves the magnificent the profound the thrilling the tragic the familiar the nostalgic the terrifying the hopeful the touching and the comfortable. I find all of them beautiful. And if those aren't beautiful enough for you, consider this: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22323.Burning_Chrome" title="Burning Chrome by William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">Burning Chrome</a> coins the word "cyberspace." <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/306.William_Golding" title="William Golding" rel="nofollow noopener">William Gibson</a> imagined it, and computer geeks made it. Can you beat that for beautiful?</span>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2537838334?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claudia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claudia" href="/user/show/20498957-claudia">Claudia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781473217447" href="/book/show/34730177-burning-chrome">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=anthologies-collections">anthologies-collections</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2537838334" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6314803557852329750">I know I am repeating myself, but after having already read Sterling, Reynolds and Gibson’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6088007.Neuromancer__Sprawl___1_' title='Neuromancer (Sprawl, #1) by William Gibson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neuromancer</a>, this collection didn’t surprise me the way I expected.<br/><br/>However, I do not contest its originality and groundbreaking impact it had at the moment of its appearance; it’s just that some books should be read at their time, especially in science fiction.<br/><br/>I still liked it a lot, though. More than 30 years after it was published, the stories seem both fresh and vintage at the same time; the audio cass</span>
  <span id="freeText6314803557852329750" style="display:none">I know I am repeating myself, but after having already read Sterling, Reynolds and Gibson’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6088007.Neuromancer__Sprawl___1_" title="Neuromancer (Sprawl, #1) by William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neuromancer</a>, this collection didn’t surprise me the way I expected.<br /><br />However, I do not contest its originality and groundbreaking impact it had at the moment of its appearance; it’s just that some books should be read at their time, especially in science fiction.<br /><br />I still liked it a lot, though. More than 30 years after it was published, the stories seem both fresh and vintage at the same time; the audio cassette is not something you encounter anymore (if ever) in this genre.<br /><br />I particularly liked <i>The Gernsback Continuum, Fragments of a Hologram Rose, The Hinterlands, New Rose Hotel, Dogfight</i> and <i>Burning Chrome</i>.<br /><br />The letdown was <i>Johnny Mnemonic</i>, which I better enjoyed as a movie.<br /><br />That being said, I believe it’s a must read for every sci-fi fan; after all, Gibson is one of the pioneers of cyberpunk.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_69988873" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69988873" />
    <a title="Kara Babcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock"><img alt="Kara Babcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580776593p2/1188122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69988873?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara Babcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara Babcock" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock">Kara Babcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780586074619" href="/book/show/583305.Burning_Chrome">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=artificial-intelligence">artificial-intelligence</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=nebula-nominee">nebula-nominee</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2011-read">2011-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=canadian-author">canadian-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=anthologies">anthologies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=posthuman">posthuman</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69988873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10245794053740980471">We are very spoiled, and very privileged, to live now in the twenty-first century. We look back on works of science fiction from the 1950s, 1960s, and onward that reference the 1990s or 2000s as "the future" and make grandiose predictions: we'll have flying cars! a eugenics war! robot apocalypse! It's interesting to note that such extrapolation, while often falling very short of the mark, tends to be conservative when it describes the technological platforms through which we acquire these flying</span>
  <span id="freeText10245794053740980471" style="display:none">We are very spoiled, and very privileged, to live now in the twenty-first century. We look back on works of science fiction from the 1950s, 1960s, and onward that reference the 1990s or 2000s as "the future" and make grandiose predictions: we'll have flying cars! a eugenics war! robot apocalypse! It's interesting to note that such extrapolation, while often falling very short of the mark, tends to be conservative when it describes the technological platforms through which we acquire these flying cars, supermen, and killer robots. The twenty-first century of the early twentieth century still involved cassette tapes and analog computers. The digital revolution is a true paradigm shift in science fiction just as it has been in the rest of our society, rendering such visions of the present future quaint. For people more open-minded than myself, this is often not a problem. I have difficulty immersing myself in stories that allude to now-obsolete technology as if it were the future—I can do it, as is evident by my enjoyment of the original <em>Star Trek</em> series, but it is difficult. I'm a child of the digital age, and I'm spoiled that way.<br><br>William Gibson is a special case. His work, too, is vulnerable to the effects of aging. Yet he is rightly called a visionary and a prescient master of this field: after all, he coined the term <i>cyberspace</i>, and his descriptions of virtual reality have influenced its depictions in film and literature ever since <em>Neuromancer</em> first appeared on the scene. So even though Gibson's stories have aged as his future never came to pass, they remain amazing and brilliant. He infused them with ideas and conflicts that continue to grip readers even as the futures these stories depict turn into alternative versions of history.<br><br><em>Burning Chrome</em> is a wonderful treasure trove of Gibson's genius. I did not like every story within, but every story is brilliant in its own way. I never liked the film version of <em>Johnny Mneumonic</em>, and the short story, though substantially different, did not change my mind. Gibson throws around some intriguing ideas, but he never really explores them with the depth I'd like. I wonder if I would feel the same way about "Burning Chrome" if I hadn't read <em>Neuromancer</em>: like the novel, it makes computer hacking into an exciting, adrenaline-fuelled experience, as the name "console cowboy" might suggest. And I really enjoyed "Burning Chrome" for the way its narrator judges the relationship between Bobby Quine and Rikki. Unlike "Johnny Mneumonic," Gibson establishes the backstory just enough to justify the main action but not so much that one feels like one is missing out on the larger picture. (But if you do, and you haven't read it, then you really should go get a copy of <em>Neuromancer</em>.)<br><br>Though "Johnny Mneumonic" is very well-known and "Burning Chrome" lends its title to this entire collection, these were not the most memorable stories for me. Those stories are <em>tame</em> compared to some of the utterly weird stuff that Gibson has displays in between them. From recorded personalities lurking just off stage to a man slowly discovering he might not be human after all, <em>Burning Chrome</em> delivers stories that demonstrate Gibson's grasp on the breadth of what science fiction can accomplish.<br><br>I'm not sure how to describe "The Winter Market." I could say a recording engineer discovers an artist who, encumbered by an exoskeleton and suffering from a terminal illness, uploads herself to a computer. That's pretty accurate, although it doesn't quite capture the nuances that Gibson infuses into the story. As the main character questions whether the recorded version of Lise's personality is actually "her" (all the while dreading the moment "she" calls him), we're treated to a flashback explanation of how they met and how her detached attitude toward life has made him dissatisfied with his own. It's interesting that so many of Gibson's protagonists are young, dissatisfied males who are down on their luck and fall in with a mysterious woman who owes him no particular allegiance: Johnny Mneumonic, Case (from <em>Neuromancer</em>), Parker, Bobby, the narrator of "The Winter Market," and Deke all fall into this category. They are certainly not the same characters—not even close!—but it's an intriguing recurring motif.<br><br>"Red Star, Winter Orbit" is one of those stories of a future that never was. Space has been largely abandoned, except for a communist, Russian space station and bubble-like domes inhabited by Americans. But Russia wants to retire its space station, which is bad news for Colonel Korolev, the first man on Mars. Thanks to an accident years ago, Korolev is unable to return to Earth and must live out his remaining days aboard the Russian space station. So when it gets decommissioned, naturally, he isn't very happy. Together with several sympathetic members of the crew, they hatch a plan to leak word to the rest of the world what Russia intends to do to its hero. It's a touching story with a nice twist at the end.<br><br>In contrast, "Dogfight" is also a touching story but does not have the endearing twist. Deke is the main character, but I hesitate to call him a protagonist. He starts low and falls farther as he seeks pre-eminence in his new obsession, combat with holographic, mentally-directed biplanes.<br><br>"The Belonging Kind" is a really weird, almost purely psychological tale about a man who meets a shapeshifter in a bar and becomes obsessed with her. I don't want to spoil the ending, although it's a little predictable, just because Gibson and co-author John Shirley do such a good job bringing it about.<br><br>However, the real star of <em>Burning Chrome</em> has to be "Hinterlands." It's a somewhat dark, depressing vision of how we might join the interstellar community. In "Hinterlands," Russian Colonel Olga Tovyevski accidentally discovers an anomaly near <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L4_and_L5" rel="nofollow noopener">an L-5 point</a>. Her space capsule disappears through it, returning years later with a catatonic Russian on board, trashed communications equipment … a seashell of extraterrestrial origin.<br><br>Boom, as they say, goes the dynamite.<br><br>You can imagine what would happen if that occurred today, except you don't have to, because Gibson describes it for us. The world's governments leap into action, and "exobiology suddenly found itself standing on unnervingly solid ground." They soon discover an awful catch to this wormhole phenomenon (which the Americans dub "the Highway"): every pilot returns dead from suicide or mad, and the mad ones usually commit suicide shortly thereafter. So why bother to pay the price of a ticket? Our narrator, Toby, explains:<br><br><blockquote>If the first ones to come back had only returned with seashells, I doubt that Heaven [the space station] would be out here. Heaven was built after a dead Frenchman returned with a twelve-centimeter ring of magnetically coded steel locked in his cold hand, black parody of the lucky kid who wins the free ride on the merry-go-round. We may never find out where or how he got it, but that ring was the Rosetta stone for cancer. So now it's cargo cult time for the human race. We can pick things up out there that we might not stumble across in research in a thousand years. Charmian says we're like those poor suckers on their islands, who spend all their time building landing strips to make the big silver birds come back. Charmian says that contact with 'superior' civilizations is something you don't wish on your worst enemy.</blockquote><br><br>To me, this paragraph shows why William Gibson is a master of the science fiction field. It's a somewhat chilling interpretation of the role humans might have if we ever enter into contact with a larger, established interstellar community: we'll be the primitive species. We won't necessarily communicate effectively or benignly, but we will acquire advanced technology and then ask for <em>more</em>, and it might very well destroy us. In <em>Star Trek</em>, despite the fact that they are the new kids on the block, humans go on to become the founders of the United Federation of Planets (along with the older, more stoic Vulcans and the volatile Andorians). Science fiction often portrays <a href="http://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Main/HumansAreSpecial" rel="nofollow noopener">humans as special</a> (warning: TVTropes), which is not surprising considering the species of both the writers and their audience. So it's refreshing when authors take a step back, think critically, and present a different perspective, even one as bleak as this: we're just rats, pushing a button to make food come out.<br><br>Toby and his lover Charmian, by the way, were rejected as pilots and now serve as "surrogates" on Heaven. They greet the returned pilots—the live ones, that is—and try to help bring them back to something approaching a normal mental state. As Toby explains, they are seldom successful. Whatever happens to pilots who go through the Highway, it breaks them. Yet "Hinterlands" concludes with Toby's laments that he and Charmian were found unsuitable for being pilots and his description of their continual longing to go on this almost-certainly fatal adventure. It's an amazing story, both in concept and in execution, and it alone is worth finding a copy of <em>Burning Chrome</em>.<br><br>William Gibson fans, put <em>Burning Chrome</em> on your to-read list if it's not already there. And for those of who you haven't read William Gibson, this would be a fine place to start (though I still recommend <em>Neuromancer</em> as well). This anthology is a snapshot of Gibson at his best, from the familiar milieu of his Sprawl world and beyond, to even weirder and more imaginative places. Gibson is a source of great ideas, and he always manages to wrap them in even greater stories.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" width="88" height="31" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380238653i/681550.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10245794053740980471" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review69988873">

<div id="review_265386574" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265386574" />
    <a title="Toby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5454475-toby"><img alt="Toby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542247337p2/5454475.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/265386574?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Toby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Toby" href="/user/show/5454475-toby">Toby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780586074619" href="/book/show/583305.Burning_Chrome">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265386574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14116440503586141724">I think this is the collection where I finally understood the cyberpunk of William Gibson despite having read four of his novels.<br/><br/>For me he is all about the mileau, the crafting of the dystopian world that his stories exist in and his characters evolve from is his primary skill, everything that comes evolves from there. Not to doubt his acknowedged talent as an ideas man.<br/><br/>I was particularly impressed with New Rose Hotel, his style of narration called to mind Chris Markers La Jetee and Wong Kar-Wai</span>
  <span id="freeText14116440503586141724" style="display:none">I think this is the collection where I finally understood the cyberpunk of William Gibson despite having read four of his novels.<br /><br />For me he is all about the mileau, the crafting of the dystopian world that his stories exist in and his characters evolve from is his primary skill, everything that comes evolves from there. Not to doubt his acknowedged talent as an ideas man.<br /><br />I was particularly impressed with New Rose Hotel, his style of narration called to mind Chris Markers La Jetee and Wong Kar-Wai's Chungking Express, the overall feel of the piece settles on melancholy without even attempting to play with your emotions or adrenaline. Johnny Mnemonic is a completely different proposition to the movie, I'm not even sure why that was allowed to happen, which was a great surprise.<br /><br />Some of the stories are a bit clunky and the better ones are co-written with somebody else but even so this would work as a great introduction to the world of Cyberpunk, much more so than jumping straight in to Neuromancer.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14116440503586141724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_151931441" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151931441" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151931441?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780586074619" href="/book/show/583305.Burning_Chrome">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-4-stars">reviews-4-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=gibson">gibson</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151931441" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3403151357878365430"><b>Wired West</b><br/><br/><i>"Burning Chrome"</i> is a fascinating collection of stories that chart the origin of the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/series/43622-sprawl' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sprawl Trilogy.</a> You can watch William Gibson building the world of the Sprawl <i>("of cities and smoke"),</i> cyberspace and the characters who would later be explored in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/series/43622-sprawl' rel='nofollow noopener'>the three novels</a>.<br/><br/>Equally importantly, you can observe him developing a unique style of writing suited to this world.<br/><br/>It's data- and sensory-rich, almost exhausting in its detail, which is revealed without information dumps or definitions. It </span>
  <span id="freeText3403151357878365430" style="display:none"><b>Wired West</b><br><br><i>"Burning Chrome"</i> is a fascinating collection of stories that chart the origin of the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/43622-sprawl" rel="nofollow noopener">Sprawl Trilogy.</a> You can watch William Gibson building the world of the Sprawl <i>("of cities and smoke"),</i> cyberspace and the characters who would later be explored in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/43622-sprawl" rel="nofollow noopener">the three novels</a>.<br><br>Equally importantly, you can observe him developing a unique style of writing suited to this world.<br><br>It's data- and sensory-rich, almost exhausting in its detail, which is revealed without information dumps or definitions. It assumes that we're keeping up with the story and we get it, without having to have things explained to us at length.<br><br>Gibson's world is a combination of the physical world, computers, data, the matrix, cyberspace, and people who use technology to travel between these substrata.<br><br>Gibson is equally adept at finding the future in the present, and the present in the future.<br><br>The narrators of the stories are often down on their luck technophiles and hackers who illicitly access the corporate segments of cyberspace. Gibson calls them <i>"console cowboys", "hustlers"</i> and <i>"industrial espionage artists"</i> who rustle data. They frequent saloons like <i>"The Gentleman Loser"</i> (named after a line in Steely Dan's <i>"Midnight Cruiser")</i> and visit brothels like the House of Blue Lights. This isn't the Wild West, but the Wired West, although it incorporates Japan, China, Hong Kong, Macao and Russia.<br><br>Not surprisingly, the style of some of the stories resembles that found in western novels, others resemble pulp or crime fiction.<br><br><b>In Recognition of a Woman's Sleeping Patterns</b><br><br>There are several narrators, all male. The women are talented, artistic, beautiful, adventurous, energetic, and mysterious, with exotic or kitsch names like Molly Millions, Dialta Downes, Angela, Antoinette, Leni, Charmian, (Colonel) Olga, Hillary, Valentina, Nina, Tatjana, Sandii, Lise (who records an album of her dreams called <i>"Kings of Sleep"),</i> Nance, and Rikki Wildside.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1456002627i/18179220.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><i>Naomi Watts in "King Kong"</i><br><br><b>Where is Your Bounty of Fortune and Fame?</b><br><br>The final story <i>"Burning Chrome"</i> contains the original use of the word <i>"cyberspace"</i>. Interestingly, it had already become the proprietary name of some computer hardware, <i>"the Cyberspace Seven",</i> which the narrator, Automatic Jack, repairs and customises so that he can access and burn Chrome's data towers with a Russian <i>"glitch system"</i> or cybernetic virus analog. Meanwhile, Jack withdraws a significant undisclosed amount of cash from Chrome's Zurich account (which stores its income from global property and prostitution assets): <br><br><i>"I watched zeros pile up behind a meaningless figure on the monitor. I was rich...<br><br>"I thought about Chrome, too. That we'd killed her, murdered her, as surely as if we'd slit her throat."</i><br> <br>Despite the female face Jack subconsciously attaches to it, Gibson's corrupt new global economy was just as dependent on data as it was on cash. As were console cowboys like Jack who knew how to hack into both in pursuit of fortune and fame.<br><br><i>"Tell me where are you driving<br>Midnight cruiser<br>Where is your bounty<br>Of fortune and fame?"</i><br><br><b>SOUNDTRACK:</b><br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br><br><b>Steely Dan - "Midnight Cruiser"</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1DJAfe7wVyM">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1DJAf...</a><br><br><b>Stuart Hamm - "Kings of Sleep"</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jvQ8BRit6ws">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jvQ8B...</a><br><br><b>Stuart Hamm - "Black Ice"</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=3UkIVRVdQGo">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=3UkIV...</a><br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_2272459771" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2272459771" />
    <a title="George" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53179837-george"><img alt="George" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456697635p2/53179837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2272459771?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="George" class="user" itemprop="url" name="George" href="/user/show/53179837-george">George</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53179837-george?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2272459771" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18411564273714164577">I seen the error of my ways. William Gibson isn't an easy author to get into and my mistake was jumping directly into Neuromancer without any prior knowledge of his writing. So from now on when somebody asks me if they should get into Gibson I will advise them to start from this anthology. It shows the themes he likes to tackle, his writing style and the worlds he likes to create and is an excellent way to ease new readers into his works.<br/><br/>Now, onward to rest of his works! </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_440904379" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/440904379" />
    <a title="Rob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10743961-rob"><img alt="Rob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422270416p2/10743961.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/440904379?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob" href="/user/show/10743961-rob">Rob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/20659156-burning-chrome">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=audio-book">audio-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=author-male">author-male</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer440904379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12825745664323656214"><b>Executive Summary:</b> An anthology of 10 short stories mostly related to or set in Mr. Gibson's Sprawl world. I enjoyed some, but not all of the stories. Only worth picking up if you really like the Sprawl books in my opinion.<br/><br/><b>Audio book:</b> 10 stories. 10 different narrators. None of them stand out one way or another. Nobody was excellent and nobody was terrible. A few did occasional voices or accents, but none of them struck as particularly memorable. <br/><br/><b>
  <u>Full Review</u>
</b><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22328.Neuromancer__Sprawl___1_' title='Neuromancer (Sprawl, #1) by William Gibson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neuromancer</a> is one of those books </span>
  <span id="freeText12825745664323656214" style="display:none"><b>Executive Summary:</b> An anthology of 10 short stories mostly related to or set in Mr. Gibson's Sprawl world. I enjoyed some, but not all of the stories. Only worth picking up if you really like the Sprawl books in my opinion.<br /><br /><b>Audio book:</b> 10 stories. 10 different narrators. None of them stand out one way or another. Nobody was excellent and nobody was terrible. A few did occasional voices or accents, but none of them struck as particularly memorable. <br /><br /><b>
  <u>Full Review</u>
</b><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22328.Neuromancer__Sprawl___1_" title="Neuromancer (Sprawl, #1) by William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neuromancer</a> is one of those books that has stuck with me 20 years later. I had the fortune of reading it before the Matrix movie came out in the early days of the internet when many of his concepts still seemed fresh.<br /><br />With this anthology you can see the building blocks of that book. Megacorporations, cyberspace, keyboard cowboys, black ice. The elements are there.<br /><br />But for the most part these stories just weren't very good in my opinion. My favorite story, Dogfight seems like it <i>could</i> be set in the Sprawl, but could just as easily be set in a completely different sci-fi setting.<br /><br />Some of the stories are "near-future" which when read 30 years later don't feel very futuristic at all any more.<br /><br />The other story I really enjoyed was Johnny Nuemonic. I guess it's a lot different from the movie, but it's been so many years since I've seen it I don't really remember. This again is one of those stories that could be at home in some generic sci-fi future as it could in the sprawl.<br /><br />The only story that is directly related to the sprawl is Burning Chrome, which I found to be OK, but not great. <br /><br />From a literary point of view, this anthology is interesting to read and see how Mr. Gibson was putting together elements of his previous works to come out with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22328.Neuromancer__Sprawl___1_" title="Neuromancer (Sprawl, #1) by William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neuromancer</a>, but in my opinion most of these stories can be skipped.<br /><br />3 Stars overall.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>Story Ratings</u>
</b><br />Johnny Nuemonic - 4<br />The Gernsback Continuum	- 2<br />Fragments of a Hologram Rose - 2.5<br />The Belonging Kind - 3<br />Hinterlands - 3.5<br />Red Star, Winter Orbit - 3<br />New Rose Hotel - 2<br />The Winter Market - 2.5<br />Dogfight - 4.5<br />Burning Chrome - 3</span>
  <a data-text-id="12825745664323656214" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_325356766" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/325356766" />
    <a title="Simon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9096819-simon"><img alt="Simon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597842388p2/9096819.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/325356766?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon" href="/user/show/9096819-simon">Simon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9096819-simon?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9096819-simon?shelf=america-north">america-north</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9096819-simon?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9096819-simon?shelf=crime-fiction">crime-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9096819-simon?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9096819-simon?shelf=postmodernism">postmodernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9096819-simon?shelf=1980s">1980s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9096819-simon?shelf=social-realism">social-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9096819-simon?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer325356766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4803108150830904688">This was... a very different read than I expected, but I liked it. I already knew that Gibson's a writer who really divides readers, and even though I generally prefer the New Wave/cyberpunk school of science-fiction over the genre's "golden age" (for reasons related to writing style rather than political ideology might I add) there were still several surprises.<br/><br/>One thing that struck me very much was how unlike the cyberpunk stereotypes the stories found in "Burning Chrome" actually are. Less tha</span>
  <span id="freeText4803108150830904688" style="display:none">This was... a very different read than I expected, but I liked it. I already knew that Gibson's a writer who really divides readers, and even though I generally prefer the New Wave/cyberpunk school of science-fiction over the genre's "golden age" (for reasons related to writing style rather than political ideology might I add) there were still several surprises.<br /><br />One thing that struck me very much was how unlike the cyberpunk stereotypes the stories found in "Burning Chrome" actually are. Less than half even qualify as tangential to that sub-genre, with a few being closer to hallucinatory magical realism and "New Rose Hotel" having so few science-fiction elements that it could just as well pass for an offbeat film noir-style crime story. Another interesting thing is how Gibson's writing contains primordial forms of the most annoying tendencies in today's Western high culture (overtly fragmented surrealistic manner of expression, a rather cynical worldview, fascination with the most dysfunctional parts of Japanese culture) but here they're actually used successfully and not at all annoying.<br /><br />This brings me to my main point: I think many people, even some of his fans, misunderstand William Gibson. While he on the surface appears as self-consciously futuristic and technophiliac as the vintage futurists mocked in "The Gernsback Continuum", in ethos he's more a Bill Burroughs/Tom Pynchon-style psychedelic post-modernist mind-bender than a "proper" science-fiction writer. Since that's the angle I read Gibson's work from, I find the so-called flaws many readers find to be my favourite things about his writing style. Likewise, the "nerd-macho" fascination with the power of technology is actually for the most part secondary to other and much more interesting themes.<br /><br />Basically, what I like about the stories collected in "Burning Chrome" is that down to fine details in the prose styles they seem to written by and for people living in the fictional worlds they describe, rather than a real-life audience contemporary to the author. It's like the readers have to "re-program" their own ways of thinking in order to get what's going on. That's a really cool way to approach fantastic/speculative literature when done right, and I think Gibson for the most part succeeds here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4803108150830904688" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I had no idea that Molly in Neuromancer was also Molly Millions in Johnny Mnemonic (I also didn't know William Gibson wrote that story! Time to watch the movie....)<br/><br/>Burning Chrome is the most significant story in this volume, because it contains most of the ideas and atmosphere that would later become Neuromancer - the cybercowboy, ICE, and the idea of viruses. <br/><br/>The other stories con</span>
  <span id="freeText17817204597622231273" style="display:none">The stories in this volume pre-date <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22328.Neuromancer" title="Neuromancer by William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neuromancer</a> by date of composition, but were published slightly after as a set. I had no idea that Molly in Neuromancer was also Molly Millions in Johnny Mnemonic (I also didn't know William Gibson wrote that story! Time to watch the movie....)<br /><br />Burning Chrome is the most significant story in this volume, because it contains most of the ideas and atmosphere that would later become Neuromancer - the cybercowboy, ICE, and the idea of viruses. <br /><br />The other stories contain similar elements - cyberpunk societies full of commercialized technology, AIs taking the place of humans on multiple levels, and altering brain function in ways we don't currently do (and, of course, more drugs!).</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15041765754059716696">This is a collection of early (1977-1985) short stories and novelettes by the master of cyberpunk, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9226.William_Gibson' title='William Gibson' rel='nofollow noopener'>William Gibson</a>. It contains two stories from Sprawl universe and I decided to read them before continuing with the Sprawl Challenge in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/474371-hugo-nebula-awards-best-novels' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hugo & Nebula Awards: Best Novels</a> group. <br/><br/>Here are the stories and their ratings<br/><br/><b>Johnny Mnemonic</b><i> 5 stars</i> this story won Nebula. It is about a man who uses his brain to transport sensitive data. Once he got data from wrong and powerful people.<br/><b>The Gernsback Continuum</b><i> 5 </i></span>
  <span id="freeText15041765754059716696" style="display:none">This is a collection of early (1977-1985) short stories and novelettes by the master of cyberpunk, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9226.William_Gibson" title="William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">William Gibson</a>. It contains two stories from Sprawl universe and I decided to read them before continuing with the Sprawl Challenge in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/474371-hugo-nebula-awards-best-novels" rel="nofollow noopener">Hugo &amp; Nebula Awards: Best Novels</a> group. <br /><br />Here are the stories and their ratings<br /><br /><b>Johnny Mnemonic</b><i> 5 stars</i> this story won Nebula. It is about a man who uses his brain to transport sensitive data. Once he got data from wrong and powerful people.<br /><b>The Gernsback Continuum</b><i> 5 stars</i>, a photographer gets a project about the US futurist architecture, “future as seen from the past” and gets quite deep into it.<br /><b>Fragments of a Hologram Rose</b><i> 1.5 stars</i>, author’s first published story, a classic ‘a boy lost a girl’ but with some future items.<br /><b>The Belonging Kind</b><i> 3 stars</i>, more horror than SF, a re-interpretation of the classic <i> Or All the Seas with Oysters</i> but with people. The protagonist, a linguistic professor accidently meets a woman in a bar, who easily shifts between styles of speech and as he follows her, see that she can change the appearance just as easy.<br /><b>Hinterlands</b><i> 4.5 stars</i> a way to travel to distant stars is found, but all returnees are either mad or dead.<br /><b>Red Star, Winter Orbit</b><i> 3 stars</i> the Soviet Union satellite and a ‘forgotten man’<br /><b>New Rose Hotel</b><i> 3 stars</i>, semi-thriller about small-time crooks who crossed serious guys<br /><b>The Winter Market</b><i> 2.5 stars</i> a new talent, whose appearance is due to the new tech<br /><b>Dogfight</b><i> 4 stars</i> flying virtual WWI planes in a depression-hit future USA<br /><b>Burning Chrome</b><i> 3 stars</i> hackers breaking ice (Instrusion Countermeasures Electronics) in their great last swindle.<br /></span>

<div id="review_5974332" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5974332" />
    <a title="Sarah Sammis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/122647-sarah-sammis"><img alt="Sarah Sammis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181271036p2/122647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5974332?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah Sammis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Sammis" href="/user/show/122647-sarah-sammis">Sarah Sammis</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122647-sarah-sammis?shelf=released">released</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5974332" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2288772372085612578">In 2005 my husband and I rented Johnny Mnemonic; it was one of the stupidest films we had ever seen. Curious to see if it was a problem with the translation to film or the source material, I decided to get a copy of the book: Burning Chrome, the first story being "Johnny Mnemonic." Having now suffered through the entire collection of stories, I can say that both the filmmakers and the author can share the blame equally.<br/><br/>I know that there are many fans of William Gibson's books but he doesn't do m</span>
  <span id="freeText2288772372085612578" style="display:none">In 2005 my husband and I rented Johnny Mnemonic; it was one of the stupidest films we had ever seen. Curious to see if it was a problem with the translation to film or the source material, I decided to get a copy of the book: Burning Chrome, the first story being "Johnny Mnemonic." Having now suffered through the entire collection of stories, I can say that both the filmmakers and the author can share the blame equally.<br /><br />I know that there are many fans of William Gibson's books but he doesn't do much for me. The worst of the stories in Burning Chrome bored me. The others were vaguely derivative of Philip K. Dick and Jack Kerouac but with some new cyber-babble thrown in. The three best stories of the book were ones that Gibson co-wrote: "The Belonging Kind" with John Shirley, "Red Star, White Orbit" with Bruce Sterling, and "Dogfight" Michael Swanwick. These collaborations allowed Gibson to world build (his strong suit) while the plot was left to the collaborator. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2288772372085612578" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_31462797" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31462797" />
    <a title="Erich Franz Linner-Guzmann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/402939-erich-franz-linner-guzmann"><img alt="Erich Franz Linner-Guzmann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392687775p2/402939.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31462797?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erich Franz Linner-Guzmann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erich Franz Linner-Guzmann" href="/user/show/402939-erich-franz-linner-guzmann">Erich Franz Linner-Guzmann</a>
      </span>

        rated it

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian " href="/user/show/30718608-brian">Brian </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=classics">classics</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3086475483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14053392204128284758">This short story collection was awesome. Everything I've been wanting in literature. "The Matrix" in literary form. Gibson creates these worlds that make you experience them as if he has taken unseen plugs and jacked you in to the story, or he has laced every one of the copies of his books with nanobots that create the experience as an interface in the brain when you read it.<br/><br/>He has this powerful, poetic style. He is a master of the craft. <br/><br/>I enjoyed "Johnny Mnemonic." Molly Millions (<i>Neuromancer</i>)</span>
  <span id="freeText14053392204128284758" style="display:none">This short story collection was awesome. Everything I've been wanting in literature. "The Matrix" in literary form. Gibson creates these worlds that make you experience them as if he has taken unseen plugs and jacked you in to the story, or he has laced every one of the copies of his books with nanobots that create the experience as an interface in the brain when you read it.<br><br>He has this powerful, poetic style. He is a master of the craft. <br><br>I enjoyed "Johnny Mnemonic." Molly Millions (<i>Neuromancer</i>) shows up and saves his ass with a death dance. He writes some stories with emotional connections on the deepest levels: failure, broken love, the tragedy of greed and selfish gain, the sensitivity of PTSD from a former soldier hanging on to hope. He writes of loneliness and the extent humans will go for love, and how far sentient beings may be able to evolve into that same need. <br><br>This collection has become a favorite of favorites, and Gibson my third favorite writer, under Tolkien and Kafka.<br><br>"It was hot, the night we burned Chrome."<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576809118i/28639533._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="14053392204128284758" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
