<div id="review_141100664" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/141100664" />
    <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius"><img alt="Deborah Ideiosepius" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425943228p2/4802663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/141100664?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Ideiosepius" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius">Deborah Ideiosepius</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/32868848-burning-chrome">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=2017-reading-challenge">2017-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer141100664" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer911846593651189734">While always hard to review short stories - some you love, some not so much- this is a pretty solid collection. <br/><br/>I first read them in the early 90's when they were cutting edge sci-fi and the inspiration for all the cyberpunk and (in my honest opinion) so very much of the new waves of science fiction, space opera and dystopia that have resurrected one of my favourite genera from the slightly fusty reputation they were acquiring.<br/><br/>These stories demonstrate the very best of science-fiction, the thing</span>
  <span id="freeText911846593651189734" style="display:none">While always hard to review short stories - some you love, some not so much- this is a pretty solid collection. <br /><br />I first read them in the early 90's when they were cutting edge sci-fi and the inspiration for all the cyberpunk and (in my honest opinion) so very much of the new waves of science fiction, space opera and dystopia that have resurrected one of my favourite genera from the slightly fusty reputation they were acquiring.<br /><br />These stories demonstrate the very best of science-fiction, the thing that draws be back and back again to the genera; 'what if' Asks the best science fiction writer, whereupon the reader finds their mind unfolding like origami in reverse as they think in dimensions they had never known were there before...<br /><br />In each one of these stories there is a premise that makes you think about humanity in a way that really is not available in mainstream fiction. In each one there is a whole background world that asks the reader to consider what our future might be - but as the backdrop to people being people. This, I feel is the most insightful of ways to ask these questions.<br /><br />So thank you William Gibson, for opening the way for the adult me to fall in love with science fiction all over again, this short story collection contains all the reasons I fell so hard the first time, and again and again...<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="911846593651189734" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3524217959" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4888561554063719517">There are books out there which still surprise me by the fact I thought I knew so much about them but in reality know so little. <br/><br/>This book and the works of William Gibson in general are a perfect point in case. I think the height of my reading was while I was at university. I was lucky enough to fall into a crowd on my course that not only shared my humour and my taste in music (now that is a challenge in its own right) but also my taste in books. <br/><br/>However one of my friends was in to cyberpunk an</span>
  <span id="freeText4888561554063719517" style="display:none">There are books out there which still surprise me by the fact I thought I knew so much about them but in reality know so little. <br /><br />This book and the works of William Gibson in general are a perfect point in case. I think the height of my reading was while I was at university. I was lucky enough to fall into a crowd on my course that not only shared my humour and my taste in music (now that is a challenge in its own right) but also my taste in books. <br /><br />However one of my friends was in to cyberpunk and the intoxicating lure of "wetware" and the promises of a fantastic tomorrow with all the promises glossy artwork and creative marketeers could come up with. We of course know now that these dates have come and gone and things still feel depressingly familiar.<br /><br />However as a result my friend was always trying to get me to read William Gibson and for what ever reason I used to find a way of putting it off. So here I am 25 years later reading the first proper book of his work and things are strangely familiar but at the same time surprisingly new and exciting. <br /><br />I know that Mr Gibson has a love hate relationship with the term but I know that his work epitomised not only a genre but also an age and strangely even though it never (or at least not yet) has been realised you can still feel the potential out there </span>
  <a data-text-id="4888561554063719517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/34745156?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/214053-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/214053-adam?shelf=literature-disguised-as-science-fic">literature-disguised-as-science-fic</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34745156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7543131406417404382">Is Gibson’s cinematic, dense, noir infused Sci/Fi best in small doses? Since this is his only collection, we have to guess. But if you want a quick welcome to his world, here it is. Vat grown assassins, criminal underground trafficking in information, razor girls, heroin addicted militarized dolphins; and that’s just the first story. There is also hints of a Gibson that could have been, with the perfect Waldrop meets Borges of “The Gernsback Continuum” and “The Belonging Kind” co-written with Jo</span>
  <span id="freeText7543131406417404382" style="display:none">Is Gibson’s cinematic, dense, noir infused Sci/Fi best in small doses? Since this is his only collection, we have to guess. But if you want a quick welcome to his world, here it is. Vat grown assassins, criminal underground trafficking in information, razor girls, heroin addicted militarized dolphins; and that’s just the first story. There is also hints of a Gibson that could have been, with the perfect Waldrop meets Borges of “The Gernsback Continuum” and “The Belonging Kind” co-written with John Shirley, a stark parable of addiction, social satire, and a surreal horror story worthy of Kobo Abe and Thomas Ligotti. Some stories are incomprehensible and others have dated elements(tapes and Soviets in space). But the sprawl stories, the ones I’ve mentioned, and the nasty “Dogfight” make it a good collection.</span>

<div id="review_197728463" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/197728463" />
    <a title="Thom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2945070-thom"><img alt="Thom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422135250p2/2945070.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/197728463?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thom" href="/user/show/2945070-thom">Thom</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17732365403369391033">This is the year of rereading William Gibson, though I read Neuromancer at the same time as a friend last year. Burning Chrome is a collection of stories, some loosely connected to Neuromancer (and the Sprawl setting). I quite enjoyed Burning Chrome and Johnny Mnemonic, both in the Sprawl. I didn't like the Gernsback Continuum. As a collection, I found this uneven.<br/><br/>It is perhaps telling that this anthology doesn't make it into the top half of anthologies I have read in the last ten years.</span>

<div id="review_134702163" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/134702163" />
    <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper"><img alt="Kat  Hooper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533675060p2/2177891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/134702163?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat  Hooper" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper">Kat  Hooper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2177891-kat-hooper?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer134702163" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9943494138985927807">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a>.<br/><br/>William Gibson is one of those authors whose style is so distinct that it’s immediately recognizable. Anyone who’s read one of his novels could pick up another and, without looking at the cover, probably identify it as Gibson’s merely by reading the first page. His popularity indicates that legions of readers love his neon-infused plastic sheeting-coated visionary style, but as evidenced by reviews of his novels at Amazon and other places, many readers jus</span>
  <span id="freeText9943494138985927807" style="display:none">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a>.<br /><br />William Gibson is one of those authors whose style is so distinct that it’s immediately recognizable. Anyone who’s read one of his novels could pick up another and, without looking at the cover, probably identify it as Gibson’s merely by reading the first page. His popularity indicates that legions of readers love his neon-infused plastic sheeting-coated visionary style, but as evidenced by reviews of his novels at Amazon and other places, many readers just don’t appreciate William Gibson. They complain about a wooly writing style and vague incomprehensible plots. Having been enthralled by Neuromancer and Count Zero, just slightly annoyed by Mona Lisa Overdrive and All Tomorrow’s Parties, and completely frustrated by The Difference Engine, I can understand both views.<br /><br />Whether you’re already a Gibson fan or a newbie who’s trying to decide if you want to give Gibson a try, Burning Chrome is exactly what you need. This is a collection of all of Gibson’s short stories which he published up until 1986. He has published only a couple of short stories since (as of February 2012). Many of the stories in Burning Chrome are very recognizably Gibson, and many take place in one of the worlds that he explores more fully in his novels. Thus, Burning Chrome is an excellent starting place for new readers and it serves to fill in some background for established fans. These are the stories you’ll find in Burning Chrome:<br /><br />- “Johnny Mnemonic” is a cybernetic smuggler. He’s got a computer chip with secret industrial research data stored in his brain and suddenly his client wants him dead. This 1981 story introduces Molly Millions, probably William Gibson’s most iconic character — the woman in black leather who has mirrored lenses implanted over her eye sockets and razorblades for fingernails. Other notable characters include the Magnetic Dog Sisters, the Lo-Tek with doberman tooth-bud transplants, and the smack-addicted cyborg Navy dolphin. (You gotta love that.) This story is way better than the unsuccessful movie starring Keanu Reeves, so don’t let that put you off.<br /><br />- In “The Gernsback Continuum,” (1981) a photographer is working with an American pop-culture expert to produce an illustrated architectural history book about the Art Deco “Raygun Gothic” style of the 1930s. At that time, Americans envisioned a future utopia that never arrived but which is still reflected in the architecture and designs of that era — cars with wings, gas stations with neon towers, beautiful happy people, and lots of chrome, crystal, and marble. As he captures these images on film, before they’re gone forever, he begins to hallucinate and, eventually, he wonders if we really would have been happy living in the type of world we envisioned back then.<br /><br />- “Fragments of a Hologram Rose” (1977) is the first story Gibson published and it introduces us to his near-future dystopian America and the “SimStim” technology that’s a major part of his SPRAWL trilogy. With the help of SimStim, Parker re-lives fragments of his history with the woman who dumped him. For a first story written while Gibson was in his 20s, this work seems quite mature.<br /><br />- "The Belonging Kind” was written with John Shirley and published in 1981. Coretti is a linguistics professor who doesn’t fit in and doesn’t know how to act in changing social situations. He often feels like an alien. One night he observes a beautiful woman in a bar who adapts her speech patterns and personality to fit in with those around her. Desperate to know her secret, he follows her for months and eventually discovers The Belonging Kind. This story is haunting and suspenseful.<br /><br />- In the SF story “Hinterlands,” (1981) humans have discovered a singularity in space through which some cosmonauts have gone and returned with highly advanced technologies, tools, or information. But those who return are affected by the “Fear” — they always go mad and commit suicide. Toby Halpert is a “surrogate,” someone who meets returning explorers and tries to keep them sane, at least until they can reveal information about what they’ve experienced. This deeply psychological story focuses on the human desire to explore the unknown, even when we’re afraid of it. It’s different from Gibson’s other work and is a good example of character-driven science fiction.<br /><br />- “Red Star, Winter Orbit” was written with Bruce Sterling and first published in 1983. Colonel Korolev, who was the first man on Mars, has been manning a Soviet space station, but the Soviets plan to shut it down and blame him for its demise. Korolev and his crew have other plans. I thought this was the dullest of the stories in this collection. Perhaps because it seems so dated, but I should note that I also did not enjoy the novel The Difference Engine, another collaboration by Gibson and Sterling.<br /><br />- We’re back to cyberpunk with “New Rose Hotel,” first published in 1984. This story introduces Maas Biolabs and Hosaka which are featured in the SPRAWL trilogy. These megacorporations compete for hot scientists and pay agents to lure them out and talk them into defecting. The world has become so lawless that they can get away with kidnapping, blackmailing, and murdering in order to get the best research scientists. The narrator of this story is lamenting the latest of these deals gone bad. It’s a touching story with beautiful characterization, and I can’t help but love the idea of research scientists being such a hot commodity. If you haven’t read Count Zero yet, I suggest reading “New Rose Hotel” first.<br /><br />- “The Winter Market” is a rather hopeless feeling tale featuring a suicidal disabled woman named Lise whose vivid dreams and nightmares are discovered by Casey, a man who edits dreams so they can be published as software. Lise’s new software, Kings of Sleep, becomes very popular, especially with the hopeless and dispossessed who can’t afford to buy it. Lise uses the money she earned to buy a way out.<br /><br />- Though I didn’t like the one novel I’ve read by Michael Swanwick, I did like “Dogfight,” his 1985 story collaboration with William Gibson. Instead of flying iron dragons, this time it’s miniature Fokker and Spad airplanes that dogfight over a pool table and are controlled with neural consoles placed behind the ear. When Deke discovers the game in a bar in Virginia, he becomes obsessed with beating the crippled veteran who’s the local champion. It’s too late when Deke discovers that there’s a high price for success.<br /><br />- “Burning Chrome” is practically a must-read prequel for Gibson’s most famous novel, Neuromancer. It was published in 1982, before Neuromancer, and is Gibson’s first work set in the Sprawl. It introduces the concept of the matrix, Intrusion Countermeasures Electronics (ICE), ICE Breakers, the hackers called console cowboys, and SimStim. You’ll meet The Finn, who’s a mysterious resident of the SPRAWL trilogy, and you’ll see the first usage of the word “cyberspace” in print. If you haven’t read Neuromancer yet, I’d suggest reading this story first.<br /><br />Story collections are a great way to get to know an unfamiliar author, and Burning Chrome is an especially satisfying collection because almost all of the stories are very good. Burning Chrome contains a nice representation of William Gibson’s cyberpunk work, but also shows Gibson’s range and ability by featuring some horror, hard and soft SF, stories written from various points of view, a couple of touching character studies, and three collaborations. Anyone who considers himself a Gibson fan should not miss Burning Chrome, and it’s a nice way for newbies to ease themselves into the strange cyberpunk worlds you’ll experience in Gibson’s novels.<br /><br />Brilliance Audio has recently produced Burning Chrome. Each story is read by a different reader, which works very well because it makes each story feel distinct. One of my very favorite readers, Jonathan Davis, disappointed me by mixing up his voices in “Johnny Mnemonic,” but I forgive him, and I heartily recommend the audio format. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9943494138985927807" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_7287613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7287613" />
    <a title="Burt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/286714-burt"><img alt="Burt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1190951444p2/286714.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7287613?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Burt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Burt" href="/user/show/286714-burt">Burt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Cyberpunk Enthusiasts, Manga Readers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/286714-burt?shelf=required_reading">required_reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/286714-burt?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/286714-burt?shelf=world_builders">world_builders</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/286714-burt?shelf=cyberpunk">cyberpunk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/286714-burt?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/286714-burt?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/286714-burt?shelf=ninjas-and-superspies">ninjas-and-superspies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7287613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16100944698349119829">I remember heading out to get this book after reading Neuromancer, solely on the strength of one story, <i>Johnny Mnemonic</i> (don't waste your time watching the movie - it's awful). While there's a lot of good work in the book, Johnny Mnemonic is really the show stopper, the tale of a 'mnemonic courrier' who can store data in wetware implants in his skull, and never know a scrap about what he's carrying. Whhatever it is he has in his head, the Yakuza wants it bad, and they're willing to send their be</span>
  <span id="freeText16100944698349119829" style="display:none">I remember heading out to get this book after reading Neuromancer, solely on the strength of one story, <i>Johnny Mnemonic</i> (don't waste your time watching the movie - it's awful). While there's a lot of good work in the book, Johnny Mnemonic is really the show stopper, the tale of a 'mnemonic courrier' who can store data in wetware implants in his skull, and never know a scrap about what he's carrying. Whhatever it is he has in his head, the Yakuza wants it bad, and they're willing to send their best vat-grown, ninja assassin after him to get it. He teams up with Molly Millions, a razor girl from the sprawl (who later appears as just 'Molly' in Neuromancer) looking to earn a name for herself in the hard, gritty life of the cyberpunk future.<br /><br />The second favorite from the book is one named <i>Red Moon, Winter Orbit</i>, the tale of a Russian Space station, slowly descending out of Low Earth Orbit, populated by forgotten scientists and buereaucrats who have little care for what has been going on on earth. As they reconcile the end of their time in orbit, they find that things have changed drastically on earth and that they may, in fact, have a new lease on live in orbit.<br /><br />Gibson's imagery is still at its most potent in these collected stories, and there is more to offer than just jaded cosmonauts and slick crime filled alleys. If the rest of his stories could keep this feeling, I think that he would have gotten a lot more attention than he currently enjoys (you either know who he is or you don't) if the work could have stayed in this phase. But, reading William Gibson can be compared to sex - it's all good, just some of it is better than others.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16100944698349119829" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2675919063" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<div id="review_37784255" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37784255" />
    <a title="Loren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/797200-loren"><img alt="Loren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1200618489p2/797200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37784255?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Loren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Loren" href="/user/show/797200-loren">Loren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37784255" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8333265159634269600">Adapted from ISawLightningFall.com<br/><br/>If the novel is a sojourn in a foreign land, short stories are trips to the municipal park. Much of their provinciality is a function of length. Long-form fiction has the space to luxuriate in detail, dwelling on tertiary characters, describing each bit of their surroundings and spawning hydra-headed plots that wriggle every which way. But while the novel remains the champion of the marketplace, it can seem downright clumsy when compared with the elegance of a w</span>
  <span id="freeText8333265159634269600" style="display:none">Adapted from ISawLightningFall.com<br /><br />If the novel is a sojourn in a foreign land, short stories are trips to the municipal park. Much of their provinciality is a function of length. Long-form fiction has the space to luxuriate in detail, dwelling on tertiary characters, describing each bit of their surroundings and spawning hydra-headed plots that wriggle every which way. But while the novel remains the champion of the marketplace, it can seem downright clumsy when compared with the elegance of a well-written short. This is doubly true when it comes to the pieces collected in William Gibson’s Burning Chrome.<br /><br />If Gibson’s first novel had a flaw, it was that the overgrowth of its imaginative setting choked out plot and character development. Burning Chrome pares back the speculative material, and the results are cleaner, better-ordered, even when they share the same world. The best-known of the bunch is “Johnny Mnemonic,” a man-on-the-run tale that reads like a genre recombination of techno-thriller, hardboiled and dystopia. (Unfortunately, most of its fame is due to being made into an execrable movie starring Keanu Reeves.) “New Rose Hotel” takes a noir-ish turn, with a mercenary specializing in corporate defections narrating the final moments of his life, sweaty hands clasped around a cheap Chinese .22. The title story comes across as an early iteration of Neuromancer, all the archetypes of data thief and cybernetic heavy and unattainable beauty in play.<br /><br />The remainder veer into different territory. In one, humanity comes in contact with a superior spacefaring species, the grim result being not exactly the stuff of Star Trek (“Hinterlands”). Another has a shy linguistics professor discovering a race of chameleon-like humanoids who can blend in with any social setting (“The Belonging Kind”). “Dogfight” and “The Winter Market” pivot on the idea of hamartia, the “fatal flaw” of classical tragedy. The former features a grifter desperate to win a championship in an underground gambling ring, the latter a wasted woman determined to become an artist in dreams before disease claims her life.<br /><br />Yes, some of the stories have aged poorly (“Red Star, Winter Orbit”) or feel more like ideas than proper narratives (“Fragments of a Hologram Rose”). But those intimidated by the breadth and density of Gibson’s Nebula-, Philip K. Dick- and Hugo-winning work should try his stories. Don’t judge them by their modest lengths. Chrome shines bright.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8333265159634269600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1425944197" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1425944197" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/207385-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301478135p2/207385.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1425944197?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/207385-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/207385-sarah?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/207385-sarah?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/207385-sarah?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/207385-sarah?shelf=cyberpunk">cyberpunk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/207385-sarah?shelf=ylto-monthly-challenges">ylto-monthly-challenges</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1425944197" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13277152390608790146">The introduction at the beginning of this collection of short stories talks about Gibson's desire for stories that are told from the bottom up. Stories which are about people in the streets and alleys of the cities as opposed to being told from the people at the top, speculating on how everyday people are experiencing life. I have t agree with Gibson. These kind of stories make much more interesting reading. I guess that's why he write stories like that too. Many of the shorts in this book are a</span>
  <span id="freeText13277152390608790146" style="display:none">The introduction at the beginning of this collection of short stories talks about Gibson's desire for stories that are told from the bottom up. Stories which are about people in the streets and alleys of the cities as opposed to being told from the people at the top, speculating on how everyday people are experiencing life. I have t agree with Gibson. These kind of stories make much more interesting reading. I guess that's why he write stories like that too. Many of the shorts in this book are about just ordinary people (if you can just have ordinary people in Gibson's worlds). They are set in near time futures, often dystopic. Low life, high tech! <br /><br />The book kicks of with the story Johnny Pneumonic. I remember seeing the film with Keanu Reeves years ago but I had no idea that it was actually a short story, let alone that it had been written by Gibson. I loved this story. It is set in the Sprawl world and Molly Millions (from Neuromancer) makes an appearance. <br /><br />I liked The Gernsback Continuum as it was different to Gibson's other stories. This story showed how he can write in different genres. It made more sense, less jardon and is the first non-tech story I've read of his.<br /><br />Fragments of a Hologram Rose had some interesting concepts but I didn't exactly understand what happened at the end. It was however very poetic and beautiful and I think it warrants a second reading.<br /><br />There were a couple collaborations in the book - The Belonging Kind was by John Shurley and William Gibson and Dogfight was by Michael Swanwich and William Gibson.<br /><br />Another Sprawl based story was Hinterlands which I loved. Quite possibly one of my favourites.<br /><br />Other stories included The Winter Market and Burning Chrome.<br /><br />Red Star, Winter Orbit and New Rose Hotel were probably my least favourites. <br /><br />I have given this book 3.5 stars. Mainly because there were a couple stories I didn't enjoy so much or understand what was happening (which is often the case with me and short stories).<br /><br />I do like Gibson's writing style so I am looking forward to reading more of his work as this book and Neuromancer are the only ones I've got to so far.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13277152390608790146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1124177908" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1124177908" />
    <a title="Blaise Pascal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37647586-blaise-pascal"><img alt="Blaise Pascal" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1124177908?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blaise Pascal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blaise Pascal" href="/user/show/37647586-blaise-pascal">Blaise Pascal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1124177908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12373540988030241153">The second story in this collection is "The Gernsback Continuum", in which the narrator is hired to take photographs of the last remaining bits of architecture from the 30's which point to the future imagined by the stories of the day -- rockets, and gleaming steel, and Utopian megalopolises. A future which never was. Hugo Gernsback, for readers not familiar, was a very influential pulp magazine editor of the time, who basically invented science fiction as a genre. He lent his name to the Hugo A</span>
  <span id="freeText12373540988030241153" style="display:none">The second story in this collection is "The Gernsback Continuum", in which the narrator is hired to take photographs of the last remaining bits of architecture from the 30's which point to the future imagined by the stories of the day -- rockets, and gleaming steel, and Utopian megalopolises. A future which never was. Hugo Gernsback, for readers not familiar, was a very influential pulp magazine editor of the time, who basically invented science fiction as a genre. He lent his name to the Hugo Awards.<br /><br />The rest of the collection, in it's own way, could be subtitled "The Gibson Continuum" -- views of a different future, now as out of place compared to ours as Gernsback's future was to Gibson's narrator. A world where the Soviet Union has a space program, putting the first man on Mars, a world where the US has fallen in influence and wealth, where Japan is an all-powerful combination of money, tech, and a shady criminal underworld. A future which never was.<br /><br />In a way, the Gibson Continuum is a mirror image to the Gernsback one. Whereas Gernsback (or his authors) saw a bright, shiny, utopia, Gibson sees a dingy, dull, distopia. American Capitalism is turned to Japanese Corporatism, Cooperation is turned to every-one-for-themselves, trust turned to betrayal, relationships to loneliness.<br /><br />I'm glad that the Gibson Continuum didn't come to be. When I first read these stories 30 years ago, the teen in me was excited about the images it portrayed, the tech it showed, the potential future where I could aspire to be like the characters in the stories. Now, when I read them, I see how flawed, how broken the characters are, I see the pain they are suffering, and how not good they are as people.<br /><br />They are still good stories, but they are dated, in terms of tech, in terms of geopolitics, in terms of world-outlook. Younger readers who can't remember the time in which these stories are written might have problems getting into this book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12373540988030241153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('nF27etIYxxdi8BNCwr+qWCQwuZ3twthxZ1NPPcgEKsk1TyEAMFuvoP0p2jxcK054IhKQW+7qr2D4ehwE4rM+pg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('F1iQ0hQr4FNetKOLCa316YU43VUkFWpcDojk3F1xQUG+Sgqo9miI5MFtavWXORHJgxr0kyc9HU2Robfld8ZVLg==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer16321548623466083670"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces' rel='nofollow noopener'>See this thread for more information.</a></i><br/><br/>William Ford Gibson is an American-Canadian writer who has been called the father of the cyberpunk subgenre of science fiction, having coined the term <i>cyberspace</i> in 1982 and popularized it in his first novel, <i>Neuromancer</i>(1984), which has sold more than 6.5 million copies wor</span>
  <span id="freeText16321548623466083670" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces" rel="nofollow noopener">See this thread for more information.</a></i><br /><br />William Ford Gibson is an American-Canadian writer who has been called the father of the cyberpunk subgenre of science fiction, having coined the term <i>cyberspace</i> in 1982 and popularized it in his first novel, <i>Neuromancer</i>(1984), which has sold more than 6.5 million copies worldwide.<br /><br />While his early writing took the form of short stories, Gibson has since written nine critically acclaimed novels (one in collaboration), contributed articles to several major publications, and has collaborated extensively with performance artists, filmmakers and musicians. His thought has been cited as an influence on science fiction authors, academia, cyberculture, and technology.<br /><br /><br />---------------------------------<br /><br /><i>William Gibson. (2007, October 17). In Wikipedia, The Free Encyclopedia. Retrieved 20:30, October 19, 2007, from <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=William_Gibson&amp;oldid=165254323">http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?t...</a></i></span>
